The actions taken by the Salt Lake City Council today demonstrate disrespect for private property rights. This ordinance is blatantly
contrary to Utah Code 17-41-402(6). This law prohibits adopting or initiating proceedings to adopt any ordinance that would prohibit

Critical Infrastructure Materials operations. Thus, it is illegal. We believe that it is not the County’s conscious intention to disobey state
law.
More alarming, the attorney for Tree Farm LLC, Brent Bateman, has sought numerous times to engage with Salt Lake County. With the
exception of dismissive emails from one county attorney, he was largely ignored.
Sadly, This ordinance will affect the property rights of not only the potential mine owner but of many others.
In the past decade, Utah’s population has grown at the fastest rate in the country, adding more than half a million residents in the last
ten years. With annual per-capita aggregate demand of around 10 tons, the rapidly growing Salt Lake City area will require 258 million
tons of various aggregates over the next 20 years. Failing to develop new, local aggregate sources will have serious consequences on
the environment and economy. Already-soaring housing and infrastructure costs will increase. Quality of life will diminish.
Additionally, this process has sadly shed a light on another serious issue: the attempt by privileged individuals in our state
... [truncated — full text is 3678 characters]

### SLCo007471 — News articles
**Element:** A-Economic Impact | **Applies to:** Claims 4, 5 | **SUPPORTS**

### SLCo007479 — MINING AND MINERAL RESOURCES
**Element:** A-Economic Impact | **Applies to:** Claims 2, 4, 5 | **UNDERMINES**

**Legal Significance:** Documents economic impact/property value diminution from regulation

> *Key Quote:* billion. The metal and industrial mineral industries paid nearly $66 million in property taxes during 2020 and over $13 million (in fiscal year 2020) in mining-related severance taxes. All extractive

**FULL DOCUMENT TEXT:**
```
Draft

MINING AND MINERAL RESOURCES
INTRODUCTION
Mineral resources provide the raw materials required to manufacture many of the products that
make modern society possible. Minerals are the source of materials used to construct buildings,
build roads, make cars, generate electricity, develop technology, and provide countless consumer
goods. Mineral resources require varying levels of processing and refining that are often dictated
by end use. As society changes and advances, additional mineral resources will be required to
fuel those changes. For instance, a transition to renewable energy will require substantial
additional production of copper, lithium, cobalt, rare earth elements (REE), critical minerals, and
other resources.
The abundant mineral resources in Utah have proven to be a great benefit to the people here and
to the entire United States for over 170 years. The production of salt from Great Salt Lake and
lime products were some of the first commercial operations begun after Mormon settlers arrived
in the Salt Lake Valley in 1847. Most of the buildings constructed after 1872 at Fort Douglas
were constructed of sandstone from nearby Red Butte Canyon, and many other homes and
buildings throughout the state were constructed of various types of stone from other quarries.1
Commercial-scale production of metals, consisting primarily of gold and silver, began in 1865.
Copper and lead production reached commercial levels in 1870 and together with the precious
metals reached a total value of over a million dollars that year.2 The late 1800s also saw the
development of Utah’s famous Bingham mining district. After the transcontinental railroad was
completed in 1869, a number of branch lines were constructed and each one contributed to an
increase in metal production that pushed total extractive industry values to over $100 million by
1917.3 Simultaneously, a number of large smelters were constructed in the Salt Lake Valley,
mostly just after the turn of the century. These new facilities helped establish Utah as a major
regional mining and smelting center by the early 1900s. Since that time, Utah’s mining industry
has continued to expand and is an important producer of numerous mineral resources.
Currently, mining in Utah occurs on a complex configuration of federal, state, and privately
owned lands. As a result, regulation and development of Utah’s mineral resources are managed
by various state and federal agencies including: the Utah Division of Oil, Gas and Mining
(DOGM), the U.S. Bureau of Land Management, the U.S. Forest Service, the Utah Department
of Environmental Quality, the Utah School and Institutional Trust Lands Administration
(SITLA), and the Utah Division of Forestry, Fire and State Lands.
Mining in Utah is primarily regulated by DOGM and their mission is to regulate the exploration
and development of coal and non-coal minerals in a manner which:
1

Powell (1994), Utah History Encyclopedia: Salt Lake City, University of Utah Press, 
... [truncated — full text is 32833 characters]
```

Monthly Newsletter

May 5 "Off the Menu" Event
Regional Development's "Off the Menu" event is starting up again! For those
who are new, these are monthly opportunities to get together as a whole office,
chat, eat, and maybe learn something!
This month will feature tamales to celebrate Cinco de Mayo and conversation
around phishing with the help of County IT.
Some might not know that Cinco de Mayo is not a celebration of Mexico's
independence. It marks the Mexican victory over the French at the Battle of
Puebla, which took place on May 5, 1862. It is still, however, an opportunity to
learn about the history, culture, food and legacy of the U.S.' neighbor -- things
that are very prevalent in our diverse fabric in Salt Lake County.

extensively in community engagement. In his free time, Adrian enjoys venturing

### SLCo008424 — PARLEY'S CANYON COMMUNITY COUNCIL
**Element:** A-Economic Impact | **Applies to:** Claims 1, 2, 4, 5 | **SUPPORTS**

**Legal Significance:** Documents economic impact/property value diminution from regulation

> *Key Quote:* Farm, LLC parcels are not designated as such. Notwithstanding Tree Farm, LLC's mineral rights presumably entitling it to this operation, many residents have expressed great skepticism that Tree Farm,

**FULL DOCUMENT TEXT:**
```
PARLEY'S CANYON COMMUNITY COUNCIL
ADVISORY INPUT AND RECOMMENDATION

November 29, 2021

To: Governor Spencer Cox
Representative Brian King, House District 28
Senator Derek Kitchen, Senate District 2
SL County Mayor Jenny Wilson
SL County Council Members District 4 and at Large
Mr. John Baza, Director, Utah Dept of Oil, Gas & Mining
Ms. Leslie Heppler, Utah Dept of Oil, Gas & Mining
Ms. Kimberly D. Shelley, Utah Dept of Environmental Quality
Greg Schiffman, ACCT President
Barbara Cameron, ACCT Secretary
RE: TREE FARM, LLC NOTICE OF INTENT TO COMMENCE LARGE MINE OPERATIONS
To whom it may concern:
The Parley's Canyon Community Council (PCCC) represents the property owners and residents
of unincorporated Salt Lake County in Parley’s Canyon, primarily in Mt. Aire and Lambs
Canyons. PCCC is a member of the Association of Community Councils Together (ACCT).
ACCT is an alliance of community councils that was organized throughout the unincorporated
areas of Salt Lake County and was authorized by county ordinance on January 23, 1985. ACCT is
a strong advocate of citizen participation and grass-root involvement and exists to preserve
self-determination for residents of the unincorporated areas of the Salt Lake County, to
represent citizens’ interests in policies that impact individuals in county, metro township, and

city community council neighborhoods and to provide an officially recognized forum with a
working relationship at all government levels thereby creating greater public awareness and
understanding of governance issues.
A November 24, 2021 Salt Lake Tribune article reported that Tree Farm, LLC had applied to the
Utah Department of Natural Resources to operate a new, massive 634-acre strip mine
immediately west of Mt. Aire Canyon in Parley's Canyon. Many alarmed property owners of our
community immediately contacted our council members asking that we provide input to
regulating authorities on behalf of the communities that this mine would impact.
Foremost, we're confused how an operation of this magnitude (~100-year life with a total area
of disturbance of 634 acres, per the application) within 6 minutes of Utah's most major
metropolitan city and in a corridor through which millions of travelers navigate each year may
be approved without any opportunity for public comment. Ms. Heppler with the Utah Dept of
Natural Resources informed one of our council members that Tree Farm's notice was to
commence operations of what is considered a "small mine" which has no allowance for public
comment. Upon further investigation, the cover letter that appears to have been submitted by
Tree Farm's attorney makes reference to it being a small mine, but the form submitted (page 2
of this link) and, more importantly, the size of the operation for which notice was being given
far exceeds (>30x) the statutory 20-acre small mine limitation when in an unincorporated part
of a county. Also, the application shows no evidence of access to water rights associated with
the 
... [truncated — full text is 6411 characters]
```

of unincorporated Salt Lake County in Parley’s Canyon, primarily in Mt. Aire and Lambs
Canyons. PCCC is a member of the Association of Community Councils Together (ACCT).
ACCT is an alliance of community councils that was organized throughout the unincorporated
areas of Salt Lake County and was authorized by county ordinance on January 23, 1985. ACCT is
a strong advocate of citizen participation and grass-root involvement and exists to preserve
self-determination for residents of the unincorporated areas of the Salt Lake County, to
represent citizens’ interests in policies that impact individuals in county, metro township, and

city community council neighborhoods and to provide an officially recognized forum with a
working relationship at all government levels thereby creating greater public awareness and
understanding of governance issues.
A November 24, 2021 Salt Lake Tribune article reported that Tree Farm, LLC had applied to the
Utah Department of Natural Resources to operate a new, massive 634-acre strip mine
immediately west of Mt. Aire Canyon in Parley's Canyon. Many alarmed property owners of our
community immediately contacted our council members asking that we provide input to
regulating authorities on behalf of the communities that this mine would impact.
Foremost, we're confused how an operation of this magnitude (~100-year life with a total area
of disturbance of 634 acres, per the application) within 6 minutes of Utah's most major
metropolitan city and in a corridor through which millions of travelers navigate each year may
be approved without any opportunity for public comment. Ms. Heppler with the Utah Dept of
Natural Resources informed one of our council members that Tree Farm's notice was to
commence operations of what is considered a "small mine" which has no allowance for public
comment. Upon further investigation, the cover letter that appears to have been submitted by
Tree Farm's attorney makes reference to it being a small mine, but the form submitted (page 2
of this link) and, more importantly, the size of the operation for which notice was being given
far exceeds (>30x) the statutory 20-acre small mine limitation when in an unincorporated part
of a county. Also, the application shows no evidence of access to water rights associated with
the 
... [truncated — full text is 6411 characters]
```

### SLCo008506 — 30 Nov 2021
**Element:** A-Economic Impact | **Applies to:** Claims 1, 2, 4, 5 | **SUPPORTS**

**Legal Significance:** Documents economic impact/property value diminution from regulation

> *Key Quote:* families? How can the justification be ”It's allowed because it’s on their own property…” when the company is unable to keep it on their own property? Consider fresh water sources: Tree Farm LLC’s st

**FULL DOCUMENT TEXT:**
```
30 Nov 2021
To Whom It May Concern,
I am writing this letter to register my opposition to the 634-acre “Tree Farm LLC” open pit mine that
is proposed for lower Parleys Canyon.
I have reviewed the 344-page Notice of Intent (NOI)1, and I believe that the filing is incomplete.
Of primary concern, it makes no mention of the approximately 100 homes and cabins that are
located near the proposed open pit mine site2. My own home, shared with my wife and children, is
located less than 1/4 mile from the boundary of the proposed mine site. That is less than two city
blocks away; almost shouting distance. Yet neither my family nor any of my neighbors were
notified of this proposal.
Mr. Jesse Lassley, Tree Farm LLC’s principal who filed the NOI, knows that the Mount Aire
community exists. How does he know? Because several years ago he built a luxury cabin in Mount
Aire – but sold it before filing the NOI. Conspicuously, the NOI fails to explain how nearby
homes might be impacted.
This mine poses a direct danger to my family, my home, and the surrounding community. The
company’s proposal states that heavy machinery and explosives will be used in operations. Per
maps provided by the company, mining activity is planned for the backside of the same bluff that our
home is built on. Might our quality of life be affected? Absolutely. To provide context, the Harper
Company rock quarry is located further down Parleys Canyon, approx 2.25 miles from our home.
Even at that distance, we can occasionally hear the dull thump of explosives inside our home. How
might explosives detonated 0.25 miles away from a residential community sound and feel? At
two blocks away, explosions are likely to shake our walls and windows. Studies published by the US
Bureau of Mines and other organizations document damage to structures located up to four
miles away from mine sites3. By my calculations, every home in Mount Aire sits within a 1.5 mile
radius of mine boundaries. Many homes are less than a half-mile away. The community’s water tank
is located 8/10ths of a mile away. The danger is real and myriad. Rumbling of heavy machinery
combined with frequent blasts could loosen rocks and boulders above homes, endangering
lives. Detonations in rock could cause liquefaction beneath homes4, resulting in structural
damage or collapse. In winter, vibrations could trigger avalanches that collapse homes, spill over
1

When this letter was written, the 344-page Notice of Intent (NOI) document was posted at
https://fs.ogm.utah.gov/FILES/MINERALS/PERMITS/035/M0350054/2021/Incoming/11122021.pdf. However, the document
disappeared from that site sometime around Dec 2, 2021, and has moved twice more since. It can still be viewed on archive.org:
https://web.archive.org/web/20211124133813/https://fs.ogm.utah.gov/FILES/MINERALS/PERMITS/035/M0350054/2021/Incoming/1
1122021.pdf
2

The NOI makes no acknowledgement of the nearby 100+ home community, aside from a mention of Mount Aire water well:
- Page 59: “General
... [truncated — full text is 23995 characters]
```

### SLCo009149 — Comments Regarding Tree Farm Mine
**Element:** A-Economic Impact | **Applies to:** Claims 1, 4 | **SUPPORTS**

**Legal Significance:** Documents economic impact/property value diminution from regulation

> *Key Quote:* e trucks traveling at various speeds with not enough lanes. 4. Impact to scenic values - the rock walls of the canyon are incredibly beautiful, only marred by the existing mine. A mine that is 4 times

**FULL DOCUMENT TEXT:**
```
FILED
JANUARY 10 2022
SECRETARY, BOARD OF
OIL, GAS & MINING
Julie Carter <juliecarter@utah.gov>

Fwd: Comments Regarding Tree Farm Mine
1 message
OilGasMining NR <oilgasmining@utah.gov>
To: Julie Carter <juliecarter@utah.gov>

Tue, Jan 11, 2022 at 7:21 AM

For you.
HB
---------- Forwarded message --------From: Joan Entwistle <joan.entwistle@gmail.com>
Date: Mon, Jan 10, 2022 at 6:56 PM
Subject: Comments Regarding Tree Farm Mine
To: <oilgasmining@utah.gov>
Dear Oil and Gas Mining Board,
I am writing to urge you to deny approval of the Tree Farm Mine in Parleys Canyon. There are many
reasons to deny this project.
1. Air pollution is a serious health problem for residents of Northern Utah, now exacerbated by
wildfires and dust from Great Salt Lake. The dust from this mine and the pollution from the trucks will
impact communities on both sides of the Wasatch mountains, as air in Parleys Canyon is blown by
westerly winds over the pass. This dust may reduce the snowpack and cost Utah millions in revenue
due to a shortened ski season.
2. Water pollution - runoff from the mine will travel into streams that support wildlife and increase
pollution of the Great Salt Lake.
3. Dangerous increase in truck traffic - I-80 is already dangerous due to the trucks traveling at
various speeds with not enough lanes.
4. Impact to scenic values - the rock walls of the canyon are incredibly beautiful, only marred by the
existing mine. A mine that is 4 times the size of the existing mine will destroy the beauty of the
canyon. The mine will be within a mile of peaks that are climbed by thousands of Utah residents
every year and severely degrade the views from these peaks.
5. Fire risk - increased truck traffic increases the risk of fires in the canyon, endangering residents on
both ends of the canyon.
6. Earthquake risk - the size of the mine could trigger earthquakes on the active Wasatch fault. An
earthquake could cause a major disaster at this site with rockfall damaging I-80 and burying vehicles
travelling on I-80.
Thank you for your consideration of these concerns.
Joan Entwistle
6169 Snowview Dr.
Park City, UT


```

---

### SLCo009428 — January 15, 2022
**Element:** A-Economic Impact | **Applies to:** Claims 1, 2, 4 | **SUPPORTS**

**Legal Significance:** Documents economic impact/property value diminution from regulation

> *Key Quote:* ed additional 140 trucks per day at peak operations Potential of a large public investments into I-80 road, exit and overpass bridge additions, expansions and new access roads to the mine Loss of wild

**FULL DOCUMENT TEXT:**
```
FILED

January 15, 2022
BOARD OF OIL, GAS AND MINING
DEPARTMENT OF NATURAL RESOURCES
STATE OF UTAH

Attn: Julie Carter

JANUARY 15 2022
SECRETARY, BOARD OF
OIL, GAS & MINING

juliecarter@utah.gov

RE: Board’s Hearing Meeting, January 26, 2022 - Parleys Canyon Proposed Mine
We are writing to let you know our concerns and issues regarding the proposed quarry mine in
Parleys Canyon.
Our Wasatch Front Mountains are one of the primary attractions to our great Salt Lake Valley.
The I-80 corridor through Parleys Canyon is a major artery for millions of travelers each year
and a beautiful entrance to our Valley and our communities.
A quarry mine in Parleys Canyon will permanently scar and harm our beautiful entrance to our
Valley. This will leave our communities, recreationists, visitors and wildlife to live with a
degraded mountainside, air quality, watershed and wildlife habitat for generations.
The proposed quarry mine is located about three miles from the mouth of Parleys Canyon and
within eight to ten minutes from the Salt Lake Valley. The magnitude of this proposed mining
operation, and in such close proximity to our Valley, is gravely concerning. We pose the
following concerns and issues about the adverse impacts a quarry mine brings.
x
x
x
x
x
x
x
x
x
x

Air quality issues and additional dust pollution in our communities
Watershed and aquifer pollution and damage; and, the water usage requirements of a
mining operations
Noise pollution from mining – blasting, excavation, crushing, vibrations and hauling
Heightened risk of wildfires from mining and trucking operations
Increased avalanche and rock slides from blasting and crushing operations
Increased semi-truck traffic on I-80 with an estimated additional 140 trucks per day at
peak operations
Potential of a large public investments into I-80 road, exit and overpass bridge additions,
expansions and new access roads to the mine
Loss of wildlife habitat and forestry destruction
Damage to the wilderness and recreational experiences many enjoy in this area
This area of Parleys Canyon is in the Forestry Recreation Zone which is supposed to only
allow development most compatible with the protection of the natural and scenic
resources of these areas for the continued benefit of future generations

We were very encouraged to learn the Salt Lake County Council opposes mineral extraction and
processing in our Forestry and Recreation zones, and ask this Board to take the same action.
Best regards,
Andrew and Marilyn Smith


```

### SLCo009477 — JANUARY 18 2022
**Element:** A-Economic Impact | **Applies to:** Claims 2, 4, 5 | **SUPPORTS**

**Legal Significance:** Documents economic impact/property value diminution from regulation

> *Key Quote:* se see below the list of my concerns and why I feel as though it is prudent for DOGM to deny an application for this operation: The size and scope of this mine is massive. It will be the largest mine

**FULL DOCUMENT TEXT:**
```
FILED
JANUARY 18 2022
SECRETARY, BOARD OF
OIL, GAS & MINING
Julie Carter <juliecarter@utah.gov>

Proposed TreeFarm Mine in Parleys Canyon
Catherine Raney <catherine.raneynorman@gmail.com>
To: juliecarter@utah.gov

Tue, Jan 18, 2022 at 12:17 PM

Dear Ms. Carter,

I’m writing in regards to the recent Tree Farm LLC application for both a large and small mine. Like
many residents (over 20,000 to date having signed a change.org petition) in the Salt Lake valley, I
have deep concerns about this mining operation.

The application alone should give many pause as to the impact that it will have on Utah’s largest
metropolitan area. Please see below the list of my concerns and why I feel as though it is prudent for
DOGM to deny an application for this operation:
The size and scope of this mine is massive. It will be the largest mine in Utah closest to the
majority population of Utah. The current operating mine in Parleys is estimated at just over 11
acres, the proposed Tree Farm mine is to be 640 acres. That means the current mine is 1.7%
the size of the proposed Tree Farm operation. The current mine has had several complaints
about fugitive dust and impact on the residents of Salt Lake City and now the TreeFarm
proposal would dramatically increase fugitive dust impacting the entire Wasatch front (millions
of people). Furthermore the proposed mine is within 2 miles of the current water treatment
plant for Salt Lake City drinking water. By way of a combination of canyon winds and fugitive
dust, there should be a high level of concern regarding contamination.
As stated in the mining application Tree Farm anticipates to move 140 trucks per day and also
stated there is only one way in and one way out of the canyon. There has been no discussion
regarding access to Mt. Aire canyon and TreeFram has not provided any mitigation efforts to
minimize access impact on the Mt. Aire community.
In regard to truck traffic, TreeFarm has stated they will move 140 trucks per day. This will have
a dramatic effect on the traffic flow and safety of I-80. Who is responsible for ensuring the
freeway and bridge access can handle the load of this increased traffic?.
As mentioned in the press and via their application Tree Farm does not “expect” any negative
implications on the Mt. Aire community, however they have provided no mitigation
demonstrations as to how they will ensure no negative implications.
There are full time residential homes that are within .25 of a mile from where explosive
detonations will occur.
They have also stated they will impact access to the Mount Aire Canyon access via the
one way in and out roadway mentioned.
Currently Mt. Aire canyon sees several rock slides and avalanches per year, there has
been no demonstration of mitigation efforts regarding increased risk exposure here.
Many of the homes receive water via natural springs, again no analysis has been
conducted to minimize any type of disturbances to the water aquifers to these homes
as it pertains to th
... [truncated — full text is 5271 characters]
```

### SLCo009553 — January 15, 2022
**Element:** A-Economic Impact | **Applies to:** Claims 1, 2, 4 | **SUPPORTS**

**Legal Significance:** Documents economic impact/property value diminution from regulation

> *Key Quote:* garding potential legal exposure from allowing the Parleys Canyon open pit mine permit to advance forward. The first area of concern comes from the enhanced risk to wildfires which might be generated

**FULL DOCUMENT TEXT:**
```
January 15, 2022
Division of Oil, Gas and Mining
P.O.Box 145801
Salt Lake City, UT 84114-5801
Att: Julie Carter
Re: Board meeting, January 26th - Parleys Canyon Mine

FILED
JANUARY 20 2022
SECRETARY, BOARD OF
OIL, GAS & MINING

Dear Ms. Carter,
I am addressing this letter of concern to alert the State of Utah regarding potential legal exposure from
allowing the Parleys Canyon open pit mine permit to advance forward.
The first area of concern comes from the enhanced risk to wildfires which might be generated from
excavating on a presently vegetated landscape at the proposed mine site. The trees and brush in all of
Parleys Canyon are seriously distressed from years of continuous drought. Two recent wildfires in
Parleys Canyon on August 6, 2020 and August 14, 2021, required the evacuation of hundreds of people
and the likely cost of millions of dollars in personnel and heavy air tanker support. The first fire was
blamed on a dragging chain behind a dump truck traveling east on Interstate 80 and the other one from a
disintegrating catalytic converter on a vehicle beyond Lambs Canyon. To the best of my knowledge,
neither vehicle or driver was located to assess the damages. I am sure that the State of Utah absorbed
some of these costs with their firefighting efforts on both fires. With such recent, proven causes for
wildfires in the Canyon, the risks from a mining operation with heavy equipment tearing up the hillside and
potentially hundreds of truck movements each day, only multiplies this exposure.
We only need to look at the wildfire near Boulder Colorado on December 30, 2021 to see the potential
risks to a cory,munity. This fire not only destroyed nearly1 ,000 homes, but also cost some lives.
Fortunately, neither the 2020 or 2021 wildfires in Parleys Canyon destroyed any structures or took lives,
but I think the result could have been vastly different had the wind be blowing in a different direction. We
were very lucky with both situations. Should a wildfire originate at this mine or along the frontage road
leading to Exit 132 in Parleys Canyon, the residents of Mt. Aire could be at great risk since there is only
one way in or out. The only other possible alternative is to drive to the end of the road and hike over the
ridge into Millcreek Canyon. This is not an attractive option for elderly people such as myself and there
are some who simply could not accomplish it. Yes, there might be loss of lives in addition to
structures. The State of Utah might be a party to any litigation if they proceed in the face of known risks.
Another area of concern for the residents of Salt Lake City is the exposure to their watershed of fugitive
dust from mining operations. This proposed mine is much closer to the Mountain Dell Reservoir than the
Harper pit. The wind frequently blows from west to east and would likely send this fugitive dust beyond
Mountain Dell Reservoir where it could coat the landscape feeding water into the reservoir. This dust
would also possib
... [truncated — full text is 4089 characters]
```

---

Pursuant to Utah Administrative Code § R641-103-100, Salt Lake City Corporation
(“Salt Lake City”), a Utah municipal corporation, submits this Petition to Intervene. Salt Lake
City seeks to intervene for the purposes of (i) supporting the December 7, 2021 denial by the
Division of Oil, Gas and Mining (“Division”) of the Notice of Intention to Commence Small
Mining Operations, Tree Farm LLC, Silver Mine, S/035/0053, Task ID# 10588, Salt Lake
County, Utah, filed by Tree Farm LLC, (ii) and opposing Tree Farm LLC’s Request for Agency
Action and any commencement of mining operations by Tree Farm LLC under a Notice of
Intention to Commence Small Mining Operations. As set forth herein, Salt Lake City’s legal
interests will be substantially affected by the formal adjudicative proceedings in the above2

Salt Lake City’s Petition to
Intervene January 25, 2022
captioned case, and the interests of justice and the orderly and prompt conduct of the
adjudicative proceedings will not be materially impaired by allowing Salt Lake City’s
intervention. This Petition is supported by the Declaration of Laura Briefer, Director of Salt Lake
City’s Department of Public Utilities in Support of Salt Lake City’s Petition to Intervene
(“Briefer Declaration”), attached as Exhibit A.

STATEMENT OF FACTS
On November 12, 2021, Tree Farm LLC (“Tree Farm”) submitted to the Division both a
Notice of Intention to Commence Small Mining Operations (“Small Mine NOI”) and a Notice of
Intention to Commence Large Mining Operations (“Large Mine NOI”). On December 7, 2021,
the Division denied Tree Farm’s Small Mine NOI. It explained that the “contemporaneous
submittal of these NOIs indicates Tree Farm LLC’s long-term intent is to commence operations
for a large mine,” and that in “the interest of administrative efficiency . . . [the Division] will turn
all focus and attention to the expedient review and processing of Tree Farm LLC’s Large Mine
NOI.” On December 10, 2021, Tree Farm filed a Request for Agency Action, seeking a
declaratory ruling from the Board of Oil, Gas, and Mining (“Board”) regarding the completeness
of Tree Farm’s Small Mine NOI and the sufficiency of its proposed reclamation surety in order
to commence operations under its Small Mine NOI.

... [truncated — full text is 24545 characters]

### SLCo009786 — January 30, 2022
**Element:** A-Economic Impact | **Applies to:** Claims 1, 2, 4, 5 | **NEUTRAL**

**Legal Significance:** Documents economic impact/property value diminution from regulation

> *Key Quote:* eight to ten minutes from the Salt Lake Valley. The magnitude of this proposed mining operation, and in such close proximity to our Valley, is gravely concerning. We pose the

**FULL DOCUMENT TEXT:**
```
January 30, 2022
To:
Governor Spence Cox
Lt. Governor Deidre M. Hulse Henderson
Senator Jani Iwamoto
Representative Brian S. King
Mayor Erin Mendenhall
Salt Lake City Council Members (7)
Salt Lake City Public Utilities, Laura Briefer
Mayor Jenny Wilson
Salt Lake County Council Members (9)

RE:

Comment Letter regarding Parleys Canyon Proposed Quarry Mine
(Aka: Tree Farm Quarry Mine, Parleys Canyon Mine, I-80 Quarry South)

Dear Utah, Salt Lake City and Salt Lake County Officers,
We are writing to let you know our concerns and issues regarding the proposed quarry mine in Parleys
Canyon.
Our Wasatch Front Mountains are one of the primary attractions to our great Salt Lake Valley. The I-80
corridor through Parleys Canyon is a major artery for millions of travelers each year and a beautiful
entrance to our Valley and our communities.
A quarry mine in Parleys Canyon will permanently scar and harm our beautiful entrance to our Valley.
This will leave our communities, recreationists, visitors and wildlife to live with a degraded
mountainside, air quality, watershed and wildlife habitat for generations.
The proposed quarry mine is located about three miles from the mouth of Parleys Canyon and within
eight to ten minutes from the Salt Lake Valley. The magnitude of this proposed mining operation, and in
such close proximity to our Valley, is gravely concerning. We pose the following concerns and issues
about the adverse impacts a quarry mine brings.
•
•
•
•
•
•
•
•
•
•

Air quality issues and additional dust pollution in our communities
Watershed and aquifer pollution and damage; and, the water usage requirements of a mining
operations
Noise pollution from mining – blasting, excavation, crushing, vibrations and hauling
Heightened risk of wildfires from mining and trucking operations
Increased avalanche and rock slides from blasting and crushing operations
Increased semi-truck traffic on I-80 with an estimated additional 140 trucks per day at peak
operations
Potential large public investments into I-80 road, exit and overpass bridge additions, expansions
and updates; and, new access roads to the mining site
Loss of wildlife habitat and forestry destruction
Damage to the wilderness and recreational experiences many enjoy in this area
Negative impact on the real estate property values, especially those close to the proposed area

•

This area of Parleys Canyon is in the Forestry and Recreation Zone (FRZ) which is supposed to only
allow development most compatible with the protection of the natural and scenic resources of
these areas for the continued benefit of future generations

We were very encouraged to learn on December 10, 2021 the Salt Lake County Council opposes mineral
extraction and processing in our Forestry and Recreation zones. We now ask that the Utah, Salt Lake
County and Salt Lake City elected and appointed officials all take this same action and support the Salt
Lake County Council in amending the County’s zoning ordinances and regulations, 
... [truncated — full text is 4262 characters]
```

### SLCo010258 — February 21 2022
**Element:** A-Economic Impact | **Applies to:** Claims 1, 2, 4, 5 | **SUPPORTS**

**Legal Significance:** Documents economic impact/property value diminution from regulation

> *Key Quote:* impacted by a mine site in such close proximity. Not just impacted in terms of property value loss – which I imagine would be significant for every homeowner – but in actual

**FULL DOCUMENT TEXT:**
```
FILED

February 21 2022

DIVISION OF OIL, GAS, AND MINING
DEPARTMENT OF NATURAL RESOURCES
STATE OF UTAH
Attn: Julie Carter, juliecarter@utah.gov

FEBRUARY 22 2022
SECRETARY, BOARD OF
OIL, GAS & MINING

RE: Comment Letter regarding Tree Farm’s Notice of Intent, for:
- February Agenda, Briefing Session on Wed, February 23, 2022
- February Agenda, Board Hearing on Wed, February 23, 2022
Dear Division of Oil, Gas, and Mining Board Members,
I am writing this letter to register my opposition to both the small 20-acre Tree Farm gravel pit and
the large 634-acre gravel pit proposed for lower Parleys Canyon. I would also like to register my
support for DOGM’s denial of the small mine operation NOI. My perspective that Tree Farm’s true
intent is to excavate a large mine in Parleys Canyon. And my belief is that Tree Farm’s filing is
inaccurate and incomplete.
I have reviewed the 344-page Notice of Intent (NOI)1, and I am concerned about the information that
has been disclosed as well as the information that is missing. Of primary concern, the NOI does not
disclose in much detail the approximately 100 homes and cabins that are located near the proposed
open pit mine site.2 My home, shared by my wife and young children, is located less than 1/2 mile
from the boundary of the proposed quarry. Several neighbors’ homes are located less than 1/4 mile
away, or less than two city blocks; almost shouting distance. Yet neither my family nor my
neighbors were notified of this mine proposal. Tree Farm LLC cannot be excused for not knowing
about the Mount Aire community – after all, the proprietor sold his cabin that he once owned there.
The NOI fails to explain how homes might be impacted by a mine site in such close proximity.
Not just impacted in terms of property value loss – which I imagine would be significant for every
homeowner – but in actual threats to homeowners’ well-being.
This mine poses a direct danger to my family, my home, and the surrounding community. Tree
Farm’s proposal states that heavy machinery and explosive agents will be used in operations.3
Mining activity is planned for the backside of the same bluff that our home is built on. Might our
quality of life be affected? Absolutely. To provide context, the existing Harper quarry is located
further away, approx 2.25 miles from our home. Yet even at that distance, we can occasionally hear
the dull thump of explosives inside our home. How might explosives detonated as close as 1400
feet away from a neighborhood sound and feel to residents? At two blocks away, will blasting
simply shake walls and windows, or worse? Studies published by the US Bureau of Mines document
damage to structures located up to four miles away from mine sites4. By my calculations, every
1

https://fs.ogm.utah.gov/FILES/MINERALS/PERMITS/035/M0350054/2021/Incoming/11122021a.pdf

2

Page 59: “...it is surrounded by National Forest System lands on the south and west and private land on the north.”
Page 195: AECOM report mentions Moun
... [truncated — full text is 27000 characters]
```

### SLCo010418 — PARLEY'S CANYON COMMUNITY COUNCIL

**Legal Significance:** Documents economic impact/property value diminution from regulation

> *Key Quote:* uld be monitored and enforced. Considering restrictions imposed by the Foothill Canyon Overlay Zone (FCOZ) ordinance, our residents question where justice lies when overwhelming scrutiny is applie

**FULL DOCUMENT TEXT:**
```
PARLEY'S CANYON COMMUNITY COUNCIL
ADVISORY INPUT AND RECOMMENDATION

November 29, 2021

To: Governor Spencer Cox
Representative Brian King, House District 28
Senator Derek Kitchen, Senate District 2
SL County Mayor Jenny Wilson
SL County Council Members District 4 and at Large
Mr. John Baza, Director, Utah Dept of Oil, Gas & Mining
Ms. Leslie Heppler, Utah Dept of Oil, Gas & Mining
Ms. Kimberly D. Shelley, Utah Dept of Environmental Quality
Greg Schiffman, ACCT President
Barbara Cameron, ACCT Secretary
RE: TREE FARM, LLC NOTICE OF INTENT TO COMMENCE LARGE MINE OPERATIONS
To whom it may concern:
The Parley's Canyon Community Council (PCCC) represents the property owners and residents
of unincorporated Salt Lake County in Parley’s Canyon, primarily in Mt. Aire and Lambs
Canyons. PCCC is a member of the Association of Community Councils Together (ACCT).
ACCT is an alliance of community councils that was organized throughout the unincorporated
areas of Salt Lake County and was authorized by county ordinance on January 23, 1985. ACCT is
a strong advocate of citizen participation and grass-root involvement and exists to preserve
self-determination for residents of the unincorporated areas of the Salt Lake County, to
represent citizens’ interests in policies that impact individuals in county, metro township, and

city community council neighborhoods and to provide an officially recognized forum with a
working relationship at all government levels thereby creating greater public awareness and
understanding of governance issues.
A November 24, 2021 Salt Lake Tribune article reported that Tree Farm, LLC had applied to the
Utah Department of Natural Resources to operate a new, massive 634-acre strip mine
immediately west of Mt. Aire Canyon in Parley's Canyon. Many alarmed property owners of our
community immediately contacted our council members asking that we provide input to
regulating authorities on behalf of the communities that this mine would impact.
Foremost, we're confused how an operation of this magnitude (~100-year life with a total area
of disturbance of 634 acres, per the application) within 6 minutes of Utah's most major
metropolitan city and in a corridor through which millions of travelers navigate each year may
be approved without any opportunity for public comment. Ms. Heppler with the Utah Dept of
Natural Resources informed one of our council members that Tree Farm's notice was to
commence operations of what is considered a "small mine" which has no allowance for public
comment. Upon further investigation, the cover letter that appears to have been submitted by
Tree Farm's attorney makes reference to it being a small mine, but the form submitted (page 2
of this link) and, more importantly, the size of the operation for which notice was being given
far exceeds (>30x) the statutory 20-acre small mine limitation when in an unincorporated part
of a county. Also, the application shows no evidence of access to water rights associated with
the 
... [truncated — full text is 6411 characters]
```

### SLCo010481 — MARCH 14 2022
**Element:** A-Economic Impact | **Applies to:** Claims 1, 2, 4, 5 | **UNDERMINES**

**Legal Significance:** Documents economic impact/property value diminution from regulation

> *Key Quote:* life of the mine has ended, presumably in 100 years, and offers $3,144,000 as a reclamation bond “for a worst-case scenario.” This is a tiny fraction of what actual reclamation

**FULL DOCUMENT TEXT:**
```
FILED
MARCH 14 2022
Dear Members of the Board of the Division of Oil, Gas, and Mining,

SECRETARY, BOARD OF
OIL, GAS & MINING

Utah Physicians for a Healthy Environment (UPHE) submit these comments on the matter of
Docket No. 2022-002- Request for Agency Action by TREE FARM, LLC for a declaratory ruling
regarding the completeness of Tree Farm’s NOI-SMO S/035/0053.
There are a handful of iconic assets, natural and manmade, that define this state, irreplaceable
assets that contribute greatly to our quality of life. The Great Salt Lake, Temple Square, and the
Wasatch Mountains are every bit as important to Salt Lake City as the World Trade Center was to
New York City. But unlike the World Trade Center, once destroyed the Wasatch Mountains
cannot be rebuilt, or reclaimed, and nothing could ever replace them. We find it shocking that
anyone would even suggest digging an enormous open pit mine in Parley’s Canyon, that would
become nearly one third the size of the Kennecott open pit mine.
There are multiple reasons why the Utah Division of Oil, Gas, and Mining (DOGM), should not
permit the proposed mine in Parley’s Canyon. The NOI submitted by Mr. Lassley’s company is
riddled with omissions and failures indicating the inability of their proposed operation, and the
ability of any similar mine, to be conducted in a way that is “acceptable” or even “tolerable” to
public health and the broader public interest
DOGM has some discretion within the law to avoid permitting projects that clearly do not serve
the public interest, and are therefore widely opposed by the public. Widespread public opposition
is clearly indicated by a petition of opposition to this mine that has gathered over 23,000 signatures
in just a few weeks, many more are being added every day. That is far more signatures than we
have had on any environmental petition in the state in the last 15 years.
UPHE joins many other groups and individuals firmly opposed to this proposal. The Salt Lake
Valley is already beset with unhealthy, chronic air pollution from multiple sources and steadily
diminishing water resources. Those trends are destined to continue and likely accelerate and both
would be aggravated by this proposed mine. More specifically, the additional dust and diesel
pollution generated by the mine will only add to the health and quality of life consequences from
existing sources of environmental degradation in the Valley. Mt. Aire residents would be
particularly exposed. And it seems nearly certain that water quantity and quality in the
area (Parley’s creek, Millcreek, groundwater, and two nearby reservoirs) will ultimately be
diminished and degraded.
The nearby homes in Mt. Aire risk damage from mine blasting, and as some residents have pointed
out, the risk of a dangerous and devastating wild fire would be increased by mine activity.
Claims of Greater Community Harm Without
the Mine are False
Mr. Lassely’s company audaciously claims that if this project is not permitted it will advers
... [truncated — full text is 25982 characters]
```

---

Representative Brian King, House District 28
Senator Derek Kitchen, Senate District 2
SL County Mayor Jenny Wilson
SL County Council Members District 4 and at Large
Mr. John Baza, Director, Utah Dept of Oil, Gas & Mining
Ms. Leslie Heppler, Utah Dept of Oil, Gas & Mining
Ms. Kimberly D. Shelley, Utah Dept of Environmental Quality
Greg Schiffman, ACCT President
Barbara Cameron, ACCT Secretary
RE: TREE FARM, LLC NOTICE OF INTENT TO COMMENCE LARGE MINE OPERATIONS
To whom it may concern:
The Parley's Canyon Community Council (PCCC) represents the property owners and residents
of unincorporated Salt Lake County in Parley’s Canyon, primarily in Mt. Aire and Lambs
Canyons. PCCC is a member of the Association of Community Councils Together (ACCT).
ACCT is an alliance of community councils that was organized throughout the unincorporated
areas of Salt Lake County and was authorized by county ordinance on January 23, 1985. ACCT is
a strong advocate of citizen participation and grass-root involvement and exists to preserve
self-determination for residents of the unincorporated areas of the Salt Lake County, to
represent citizens’ interests in policies that impact individuals in county, metro township, and

city community council neighborhoods and to provide an officially recognized forum with a
working relationship at all government levels thereby creating greater public awareness and
understanding of governance issues.
A November 24, 2021 Salt Lake Tribune article reported that Tree Farm, LLC had applied to the
Utah Department of Natural Resources to operate a new, massive 634-acre strip mine
immediately west of Mt. Aire Canyon in Parley's Canyon. Many alarmed property owners of our
community immediately contacted our council members asking that we provide input to
regulating authorities on behalf of the communities that this mine would impact.
Foremost, we're confused how an operation of this magnitude (~100-year life with a total area
of disturbance of 634 acres, per the application) within 6 minutes of Utah's most major
metropolitan city and in a corridor through which millions of travelers navigate each year may
be approved without any opportunity for public comment. Ms. Heppler with the Utah Dept of
Natural Resources informed one of our council members that Tree Farm's notice was to
commence operations of what is considered a "small mine" which has no allowance for public
comment. Upon further investigation, the cover letter that appears to have been submitted by
Tree Farm's attorney makes reference to it being a small mine, but the form submitted (page 2
of this link) and, more importantly, the size of the operation for which notice was being given
far exceeds (>30x) the statutory 20-acre small mine limitation when in an unincorporated part
of a county. Also, the application shows no evidence of access to water rights associated with
the 
... [truncated — full text is 6411 characters]

my husband did graduate work in Manual Therapy, we have lived in and LOVED our UTAH MOUNTAIN HOMES for
almost 50 years together! Our adult children represent this generation who make the great out of doors their LIFESTYLE,
not just a planned occasional hike or ski day, but a way of interfacing with the natural world that surrounds Salt Lake
valley as part of the very air they breathe! And that air is getting very polluted and would be even more compromised by
the working of a 650 acre open pit mine with 150 trucks a day spewing their exhaust into our already detrimentally
affected air!!!!

Division’s Motion for Police Guidance and
Tree Farm, LLC’s proposed quarry gravel pit in lower Parleys Canyon.

Dear Members of the Division and the Board,
I am writing in regards to recent actions taken on Docket No. 2022-002-Request for Agency Action by Tree Farm
LLC. They have withdrawn both the SMO and LMO Notices of Intent and will be moving to re-file a new SMO
NOI in the near future. Tree Farm cannot hide the fact that their long-term intention and goal is to operate a large
gravel pit mining operation on 634 acres in the lower part of Parleys Canyon. If Tree Farm begins with a newly
filed SMO NOI, it is easy to see this is a shifty tactic and attempt to expand into a LMO in the near future.
I ask the Division and the Board to move forward quickly to make changes to mining policies and rules based on
the ideas and suggestions in the “Motion for Policy Guidance”. To do so, is in the best interest of the State and the
required planning, policies and infrastructure needed for the projected population growth in Utah’s future.
FILED

MARCH 22 2022

SECRETARY, BOARD OF
OIL, GAS & MINING

I also ask the Division to enact a moratorium on all SMO and LMO NOIs in Class 1 and Class 2 counties until a
thorough examination and a broader study of all the issues and facts have been completed and resolved. Time is
needed to make the desired changes in policies and rules regulating mining operations in the State.
There is much opposition to a gravel pit in Parleys Canyon from citizens, communities, government agencies and
environmental groups. Many have voiced serious concerns about issues and negative impacts any mining operation
in our local Forestry and Recreation Zones in the Wasatch Mountains will have on our lives, communities and
environment. My letter is not to repeat those concerns and arguments, only to offer some additional and related
information and reasons for your consideration.
In the Division’s recent “Motion for Policy Guidance” filed March 17, 2022, it requests guidance from the Board
concerning alternative solutions to the “Cause” and possible legislative or rulemaking changes. The document
begins with the projected population growth in Utah and states: “will increase by 66% over the next four decades”
and references a current 2021 study. I looked at this and other studies and have some additional points to consider
as you look inside the numbers.
1) Yes, Utah is one of the top growth states and there will be a continual need for mined materials; however, the
66% growth figure over the next four decades equates to an average annual rate of 1.65%. Looking inside the
numbers, projections and historical data brings helpful information to one’s analysis.

### SLCo011066 — MINING AND MINERAL RESOURCES
**Element:** A-Economic Impact | **Applies to:** Claims 2, 3, 4, 5 | **SUPPORTS**

**Legal Significance:** Documents economic impact/property value diminution from regulation

> *Key Quote:* nue to come primarily from Utah’s general fund but are supplemented by a modest permit fee program implemented in 1998. DOGM’s Abandoned Mine Reclamation Program (AMRP) c

**FULL DOCUMENT TEXT:**
```
Draft

MINING AND MINERAL RESOURCES
INTRODUCTION
Mineral resources provide the raw materials required to manufacture many of the products that
make modern society possible. Minerals are the source of materials used to construct buildings,
build roads, make cars, generate electricity, develop technology, and provide countless consumer
goods. Mineral resources require varying levels of processing and refining that are often dictated
by end use. As society changes and advances, additional mineral resources will be required to
fuel those changes. For instance, a transition to renewable energy will require substantial
additional production of copper, lithium, cobalt, rare earth elements (REE), critical minerals, and
other resources.
The abundant mineral resources in Utah have proven to be a great benefit to the people here and
to the entire United States for over 170 years. The production of salt from Great Salt Lake and
lime products were some of the first commercial operations begun after Mormon settlers arrived
in the Salt Lake Valley in 1847. Most of the buildings constructed after 1872 at Fort Douglas
were constructed of sandstone from nearby Red Butte Canyon, and many other homes and
buildings throughout the state were constructed of various types of stone from other quarries.1
Commercial-scale production of metals, consisting primarily of gold and silver, began in 1865.
Copper and lead production reached commercial levels in 1870 and together with the precious
metals reached a total value of over a million dollars that year.2 The late 1800s also saw the
development of Utah’s famous Bingham mining district. After the transcontinental railroad was
completed in 1869, a number of branch lines were constructed and each one contributed to an
increase in metal production that pushed total extractive industry values to over $100 million by
1917.3 Simultaneously, a number of large smelters were constructed in the Salt Lake Valley,
mostly just after the turn of the century. These new facilities helped establish Utah as a major
regional mining and smelting center by the early 1900s. Since that time, Utah’s mining industry
has continued to expand and is an important producer of numerous mineral resources.
Currently, mining in Utah occurs on a complex configuration of federal, state, and privately
owned lands. As a result, regulation and development of Utah’s mineral resources are managed
by various state and federal agencies including: the Utah Division of Oil, Gas and Mining
(DOGM), the U.S. Bureau of Land Management, the U.S. Forest Service, the Utah Department
of Environmental Quality, the Utah School and Institutional Trust Lands Administration
(SITLA), and the Utah Division of Forestry, Fire and State Lands.
Mining in Utah is primarily regulated by DOGM and their mission is to regulate the exploration
and development of coal and non-coal minerals in a manner which:
1

Powell (1994), Utah History Encyclopedia: Salt Lake City, University of Utah Press, 
... [truncated — full text is 32833 characters]
```

Regional Development
Monthly Newsletter

May 5 "Off the Menu" Event
Regional Development's "Off the Menu" event is starting up again! For those
who are new, these are monthly opportunities to get together as a whole office,
chat, eat, and maybe learn something!
This month will feature tamales to celebrate Cinco de Mayo and conversation
around phishing with the help of County IT.
Some might not know that Cinco de Mayo is not a celebration of Mexico's
independence. It marks the Mexican victory over the French at the Battle of
Puebla, which took place on May 5, 1862. It is still, however, an opportunity to
learn about the history, culture, food and legacy of the U.S.' neighbor -- things
that are very prevalent in our diverse fabric in Salt Lake County.
Off the Menu

Where: Government Center Cafeteria (1st floor, south building)

as ORD's Business Strategy Manager. Before the County, Saskia helped
other countries conduct census; ask her about collecting demographic data!
One thing you might not already know about Saskia is after college, she
bicycled across the entire U.S. with an organization called Bike & Build. They
biked 6 days a week and during days off worked with Habitat for Humanity.
When she's not focused on data, she loves watching MASH.

Welcome, Adrian West!
Adrian joined the Economic Development team as the Municipal Economic
Development Manager in March, where he helps develop and implement
economic develop strategies for the metro townships in Salt Lake County. A
large part of the role involves understanding the needs of small businesses and
local stakeholders to inform economic development decisions. He works
extensively in community engagement. In his free time, Adrian enjoys venturing

outdoors, reading books on business and management, and exercising. He
holds an MBA in Technology Commercialization from Westminster College and
a BS in Peace and Conflict Studies from the University of Utah.

### SLCo011149 — 30 Nov 2021
**Element:** A-Economic Impact | **Applies to:** Claims 1, 2, 3, 4, 5 | **SUPPORTS**

**Legal Significance:** Documents economic impact/property value diminution from regulation

> *Key Quote:* roximately 1/4 mile down the canyon, exposed to mining activity. Maps on the US Geological Society’s website clearly display this open creek bed, and the Interactive Map

**FULL DOCUMENT TEXT:**
```
30 Nov 2021
To Whom It May Concern,
I am writing this letter to register my opposition to the 634-acre “Tree Farm LLC” open pit mine that
is proposed for lower Parleys Canyon.
I have reviewed the 344-page Notice of Intent (NOI)1, and I believe that the filing is incomplete.
Of primary concern, it makes no mention of the approximately 100 homes and cabins that are
located near the proposed open pit mine site2. My own home, shared with my wife and children, is
located less than 1/4 mile from the boundary of the proposed mine site. That is less than two city
blocks away; almost shouting distance. Yet neither my family nor any of my neighbors were
notified of this proposal.
Mr. Jesse Lassley, Tree Farm LLC’s principal who filed the NOI, knows that the Mount Aire
community exists. How does he know? Because several years ago he built a luxury cabin in Mount
Aire – but sold it before filing the NOI. Conspicuously, the NOI fails to explain how nearby
homes might be impacted.
This mine poses a direct danger to my family, my home, and the surrounding community. The
company’s proposal states that heavy machinery and explosives will be used in operations. Per
maps provided by the company, mining activity is planned for the backside of the same bluff that our
home is built on. Might our quality of life be affected? Absolutely. To provide context, the Harper
Company rock quarry is located further down Parleys Canyon, approx 2.25 miles from our home.
Even at that distance, we can occasionally hear the dull thump of explosives inside our home. How
might explosives detonated 0.25 miles away from a residential community sound and feel? At
two blocks away, explosions are likely to shake our walls and windows. Studies published by the US
Bureau of Mines and other organizations document damage to structures located up to four
miles away from mine sites3. By my calculations, every home in Mount Aire sits within a 1.5 mile
radius of mine boundaries. Many homes are less than a half-mile away. The community’s water tank
is located 8/10ths of a mile away. The danger is real and myriad. Rumbling of heavy machinery
combined with frequent blasts could loosen rocks and boulders above homes, endangering
lives. Detonations in rock could cause liquefaction beneath homes4, resulting in structural
damage or collapse. In winter, vibrations could trigger avalanches that collapse homes, spill over
1

When this letter was written, the 344-page Notice of Intent (NOI) document was posted at
https://fs.ogm.utah.gov/FILES/MINERALS/PERMITS/035/M0350054/2021/Incoming/11122021.pdf. However, the document
disappeared from that site sometime around Dec 2, 2021, and has moved twice more since. It can still be viewed on archive.org:
https://web.archive.org/web/20211124133813/https://fs.ogm.utah.gov/FILES/MINERALS/PERMITS/035/M0350054/2021/Incoming/1
1122021.pdf
2

The NOI makes no acknowledgement of the nearby 100+ home community, aside from a mention of Mount Aire water well:
- Page 59: “General
... [truncated — full text is 23995 characters]
```

### SLCo011323 — January 30, 2022
**Element:** A-Economic Impact | **Applies to:** Claims 1, 2, 3, 4, 5 | **NEUTRAL**

**Legal Significance:** Documents economic impact/property value diminution from regulation

> *Key Quote:* eight to ten minutes from the Salt Lake Valley. The magnitude of this proposed mining operation, and in such close proximity to our Valley, is gravely concerning. We pose the

**FULL DOCUMENT TEXT:**
```
January 30, 2022
To:
Governor Spence Cox
Lt. Governor Deidre M. Hulse Henderson
Senator Jani Iwamoto
Representative Brian S. King
Mayor Erin Mendenhall
Salt Lake City Council Members (7)
Salt Lake City Public Utilities, Laura Briefer
Mayor Jenny Wilson
Salt Lake County Council Members (9)

RE:

Comment Letter regarding Parleys Canyon Proposed Quarry Mine
(Aka: Tree Farm Quarry Mine, Parleys Canyon Mine, I-80 Quarry South)

Dear Utah, Salt Lake City and Salt Lake County Officers,
We are writing to let you know our concerns and issues regarding the proposed quarry mine in Parleys
Canyon.
Our Wasatch Front Mountains are one of the primary attractions to our great Salt Lake Valley. The I-80
corridor through Parleys Canyon is a major artery for millions of travelers each year and a beautiful
entrance to our Valley and our communities.
A quarry mine in Parleys Canyon will permanently scar and harm our beautiful entrance to our Valley.
This will leave our communities, recreationists, visitors and wildlife to live with a degraded
mountainside, air quality, watershed and wildlife habitat for generations.
The proposed quarry mine is located about three miles from the mouth of Parleys Canyon and within
eight to ten minutes from the Salt Lake Valley. The magnitude of this proposed mining operation, and in
such close proximity to our Valley, is gravely concerning. We pose the following concerns and issues
about the adverse impacts a quarry mine brings.
•
•
•
•
•
•
•
•
•
•

Air quality issues and additional dust pollution in our communities
Watershed and aquifer pollution and damage; and, the water usage requirements of a mining
operations
Noise pollution from mining – blasting, excavation, crushing, vibrations and hauling
Heightened risk of wildfires from mining and trucking operations
Increased avalanche and rock slides from blasting and crushing operations
Increased semi-truck traffic on I-80 with an estimated additional 140 trucks per day at peak
operations
Potential large public investments into I-80 road, exit and overpass bridge additions, expansions
and updates; and, new access roads to the mining site
Loss of wildlife habitat and forestry destruction
Damage to the wilderness and recreational experiences many enjoy in this area
Negative impact on the real estate property values, especially those close to the proposed area

•

This area of Parleys Canyon is in the Forestry and Recreation Zone (FRZ) which is supposed to only
allow development most compatible with the protection of the natural and scenic resources of
these areas for the continued benefit of future generations

We were very encouraged to learn on December 10, 2021 the Salt Lake County Council opposes mineral
extraction and processing in our Forestry and Recreation zones. We now ask that the Utah, Salt Lake
County and Salt Lake City elected and appointed officials all take this same action and support the Salt
Lake County Council in amending the County’s zoning ordinances and regulations, 
... [truncated — full text is 4262 characters]
```

---

### SLCo011350 — RE: Meeting Request
**Element:** A-Economic Impact | **Applies to:** Claims 1, 2, 3, 4, 5 | **NEUTRAL**

**Legal Significance:** Documents economic impact/property value diminution from regulation

> *Key Quote:* arry planned for Parleys Canyon. This started the process to amend the county’s zoning ordinances to prohibit new quarries from being developed in the Wasatch foothills. There is concern th

**FULL DOCUMENT TEXT:**
```
From: Christie Morgan <CMorgan@slco.org>
To: Aimee Winder Newton <ANewton@slco.org>
Subject: RE: Meeting Request
Date: Fri, 18 Feb 2022 12:57:11 -0700
Importance: Normal
Inline-Images: image001.png

This is in regard to the special session back in December that was the first step to block a limestone quarry planned for
Parleys Canyon. This started the process to amend the county’s zoning ordinances to prohibit new quarries from being
developed in the Wasatch foothills. There is concern that this may conflict with a law passed in 2019 prohibiting counties
from restricting the extraction of aggregate and other “critical infrastructure materials.”
A company called Tree Farm wants to use drills and heavy explosives in Parleys Canyon near Grandeur Peak to pull out 2
million tons of crushed limestone each year. Tree Farm is a Utah company registered to developer Jesse Lassley, who
previously owned a home in the neighboring Mt. Aire Canyon. The plan was brought to the council’s attention in
November after the company submitted a notice of intent to the Utah Division of Oil, Gas and Mining.
Homeowners in Mt. Aire Canyon organized an opposition campaign and have a website https://saveparleys.org/
Tree Farm’s application for a small mine was denied, reasoning that the company’s true intent is to develop a large mine,
as opposed to the maximum 20-acre footprint allowed under a small-mine permit.
Tree Farm disputed that decision, and an appeal will be heard Feb. 23. Salt Lake City and Millcreek are asking that Tree
Farm should not be allowed to circumvent the state’s more robust permitting procedure for large mines.
Tree Farm has named Granite Construction as their operator and are calling the location the I-80 South Quarry. They have
a website https://www.i80southquarry.com/
Here are links to a couple of SLTrib articles:
https://www.sltrib.com/news/environment/2021/12/10/salt-lake-county-moves/
https://www.sltrib.com/news/environment/2022/01/29/granite-construction/
Let me know if you’d like more information and I can keep researching!
Christie
From: Aimee Winder Newton <ANewton@slco.org>
Sent: Thursday, February 17, 2022 6:26 PM
To: Christie Morgan <CMorgan@slco.org>
Subject: Re: Meeting Request
Great! Any info you can dig up on this for me so I’m prepared for this meeting would be great!
Aimee Winder Newton
Salt Lake County Council

vulnerability of the foothill and canyon areas to this type of development. We’re relieved that the proposed ordinance
change has been set in motion. But we also have concerns around potential state legislative intervention, among other
concerns. I’d love to learn how Councilwoman Winder Newton thinks about these vul
... [truncated — full text is 4592 characters]

### SLCo011761 — PARLEY'S CANYON COMMUNITY COUNCIL
**Element:** A-Economic Impact | **Applies to:** Claims 1, 2, 3, 4, 5 | **UNDERMINES**

**Legal Significance:** Documents economic impact/property value diminution from regulation

> *Key Quote:* uld be monitored and enforced. Considering restrictions imposed by the Foothill Canyon Overlay Zone (FCOZ) ordinance, our residents question where justice lies when overwhelming scrutiny is applie

**FULL DOCUMENT TEXT:**
```
PARLEY'S CANYON COMMUNITY COUNCIL
ADVISORY INPUT AND RECOMMENDATION

November 29, 2021

To: Governor Spencer Cox
Representative Brian King, House District 28
Senator Derek Kitchen, Senate District 2
SL County Mayor Jenny Wilson
SL County Council Members District 4 and at Large
Mr. John Baza, Director, Utah Dept of Oil, Gas & Mining
Ms. Leslie Heppler, Utah Dept of Oil, Gas & Mining
Ms. Kimberly D. Shelley, Utah Dept of Environmental Quality
Greg Schiffman, ACCT President
Barbara Cameron, ACCT Secretary
RE: TREE FARM, LLC NOTICE OF INTENT TO COMMENCE LARGE MINE OPERATIONS
To whom it may concern:
The Parley's Canyon Community Council (PCCC) represents the property owners and residents
of unincorporated Salt Lake County in Parley’s Canyon, primarily in Mt. Aire and Lambs
Canyons. PCCC is a member of the Association of Community Councils Together (ACCT).
ACCT is an alliance of community councils that was organized throughout the unincorporated
areas of Salt Lake County and was authorized by county ordinance on January 23, 1985. ACCT is
a strong advocate of citizen participation and grass-root involvement and exists to preserve
self-determination for residents of the unincorporated areas of the Salt Lake County, to
represent citizens’ interests in policies that impact individuals in county, metro township, and

city community council neighborhoods and to provide an officially recognized forum with a
working relationship at all government levels thereby creating greater public awareness and
understanding of governance issues.
A November 24, 2021 Salt Lake Tribune article reported that Tree Farm, LLC had applied to the
Utah Department of Natural Resources to operate a new, massive 634-acre strip mine
immediately west of Mt. Aire Canyon in Parley's Canyon. Many alarmed property owners of our
community immediately contacted our council members asking that we provide input to
regulating authorities on behalf of the communities that this mine would impact.
Foremost, we're confused how an operation of this magnitude (~100-year life with a total area
of disturbance of 634 acres, per the application) within 6 minutes of Utah's most major
metropolitan city and in a corridor through which millions of travelers navigate each year may
be approved without any opportunity for public comment. Ms. Heppler with the Utah Dept of
Natural Resources informed one of our council members that Tree Farm's notice was to
commence operations of what is considered a "small mine" which has no allowance for public
comment. Upon further investigation, the cover letter that appears to have been submitted by
Tree Farm's attorney makes reference to it being a small mine, but the form submitted (page 2
of this link) and, more importantly, the size of the operation for which notice was being given
far exceeds (>30x) the statutory 20-acre small mine limitation when in an unincorporated part
of a county. Also, the application shows no evidence of access to water rights associated with
the 
... [truncated — full text is 6411 characters]
```

---

### SLCo012905 — Comments Regarding Tree Farm Mine
**Element:** A-Economic Impact | **Applies to:** Claims 3, 4 | **SUPPORTS**

**Legal Significance:** Documents economic impact/property value diminution from regulation

> *Key Quote:* e trucks traveling at
various speeds with not enough lanes.
4. Impact to scenic values - the rock walls of the canyon are incredibly beautiful, only marred by the
existing mine. A mine that is 4 times

**FULL DOCUMENT TEXT:**
```
FILED
JANUARY 10 2022
SECRETARY, BOARD OF
OIL, GAS & MINING
Julie Carter <juliecarter@utah.gov>

Fwd: Comments Regarding Tree Farm Mine
1 message
OilGasMining NR <oilgasmining@utah.gov>
To: Julie Carter <juliecarter@utah.gov>

Tue, Jan 11, 2022 at 7:21 AM

For you.
HB
---------- Forwarded message --------From: Joan Entwistle <joan.entwistle@gmail.com>
Date: Mon, Jan 10, 2022 at 6:56 PM
Subject: Comments Regarding Tree Farm Mine
To: <oilgasmining@utah.gov>
Dear Oil and Gas Mining Board,
I am writing to urge you to deny approval of the Tree Farm Mine in Parleys Canyon. There are many
reasons to deny this project.
1. Air pollution is a serious health problem for residents of Northern Utah, now exacerbated by
wildfires and dust from Great Salt Lake. The dust from this mine and the pollution from the trucks will
impact communities on both sides of the Wasatch mountains, as air in Parleys Canyon is blown by
westerly winds over the pass. This dust may reduce the snowpack and cost Utah millions in revenue
due to a shortened ski season.
2. Water pollution - runoff from the mine will travel into streams that support wildlife and increase
pollution of the Great Salt Lake.
3. Dangerous increase in truck traffic - I-80 is already dangerous due to the trucks traveling at
various speeds with not enough lanes.
4. Impact to scenic values - the rock walls of the canyon are incredibly beautiful, only marred by the
existing mine. A mine that is 4 times the size of the existing mine will destroy the beauty of the
canyon. The mine will be within a mile of peaks that are climbed by thousands of Utah residents
every year and severely degrade the views from these peaks.
5. Fire risk - increased truck traffic increases the risk of fires in the canyon, endangering residents on
both ends of the canyon.
6. Earthquake risk - the size of the mine could trigger earthquakes on the active Wasatch fault. An
earthquake could cause a major disaster at this site with rockfall damaging I-80 and burying vehicles
travelling on I-80.
Thank you for your consideration of these concerns.
Joan Entwistle
6169 Snowview Dr.
Park City, UT


```

---

### SLCo012931 — January 15, 2022

```
FILED

January 15, 2022
BOARD OF OIL, GAS AND MINING
DEPARTMENT OF NATURAL RESOURCES
STATE OF UTAH

Attn: Julie Carter

JANUARY 15 2022
SECRETARY, BOARD OF
OIL, GAS & MINING

juliecarter@utah.gov

RE: Board’s Hearing Meeting, January 26, 2022 - Parleys Canyon Proposed Mine
We are writing to let you know our concerns and issues regarding the proposed quarry mine in
Parleys Canyon.
Our Wasatch Front Mountains are one of the primary attractions to our great Salt Lake Valley.
The I-80 corridor through Parleys Canyon is a major artery for millions of travelers each year
and a beautiful entrance to our Valley and our communities.
A quarry mine in Parleys Canyon will permanently scar and harm our beautiful entrance to our
Valley. This will leave our communities, recreationists, visitors and wildlife to live with a
degraded mountainside, air quality, watershed and wildlife habitat for generations.
The proposed quarry mine is located about three miles from the mouth of Parleys Canyon and
within eight to ten minutes from the Salt Lake Valley. The magnitude of this proposed mining
operation, and in such close proximity to our Valley, is gravely concerning. We pose the
following concerns and issues about the adverse impacts a quarry mine brings.
x
x
x
x
x
x
x
x
x
x

Air quality issues and additional dust pollution in our communities
Watershed and aquifer pollution and damage; and, the water usage requirements of a
mining operations
Noise pollution from mining – blasting, excavation, crushing, vibrations and hauling
Heightened risk of wildfires from mining and trucking operations
Increased avalanche and rock slides from blasting and crushing operations
Increased semi-truck traffic on I-80 with an estimated additional 140 trucks per day at
peak operations
Potential of a large public investments into I-80 road, exit and overpass bridge additions,
expansions and new access roads to the mine
Loss of wildlife habitat and forestry destruction
Damage to the wilderness and recreational experiences many enjoy in this area
This area of Parleys Canyon is in the Forestry Recreation Zone which is supposed to only
allow development most compatible with the protection of the natural and scenic
resources of these areas for the continued benefit of future generations

We were very encouraged to learn the Salt Lake County Council opposes mineral extraction and
processing in our Forestry and Recreation zones, and ask this Board to take the same action.
Best regards,
Andrew and Marilyn Smith


```

**FULL DOCUMENT TEXT:**

‘This is long overdue’ —
More trails are coming to
the Oquirrh Mountains.
See where.
Salt Lake County’s plan would change the shape of
recreation not only for the west side but also be relieving
pressure on the crowded Wasatch canyons.

(Francisco Kjolseth | The Salt Lake Tribune) The west bench near Herriman is expected to gain more
trails, expanding the Butterfield Canyon, Yellow Fork and Rose Canyons Recreation Area as seen on
Wednesday, March 16, 2022.

By Alixel Cabrera
| March 23, 2022, 7:00 a.m.

Looking west of Herriman offers a graceful view of the Oquirrh Mountains.
They look peaceful and give a glimpse of open spaces. Much of the area has
been home to mining for decades, but planners envision a different, cleaner
environmental future — by bringing more foot traffic to the foothills.
How? Trails. Lots of them.
Expanding the trail system in the Oquirrhs — from Magna in the north to
Herriman in the south — will not only provide more outdoor options to westsiders but also relieve pressure to the east, where the Wasatch Mountains are
often packed with year-round recreationists.
“This is long overdue,” Salt Lake County Council member Richard Snelgrove
said, “having mountain outdoor recreational opportunities for those that live
on the west side or anywhere in the Salt Lake Valley.”
Expanding Rose and Yellow Fork trails

(Francisco Kjolseth | The Salt Lake Tribune) The west bench near Herriman is expected
to gain more trails, expanding the Yellow Fork and Canyons Recreation Area as seen on
Wednesday, March 16, 2022.
Many of the planned trails would be in Yellow Fork and Rose canyons, according to the
county’s draft plan for the west bench. The current trails draw hikers, runners, cyclists
and horseback riders.
At a later stage, Butterfield Canyon would be connected to Yellow Fork and Rose
canyons. The projects would be paid for through tourism, recreation, culture and
convention funds, said Walt Gilmore, associate division director of planning and
development for county Parks and Recreation.
The county is working with the Bureau of Land Management and Rio Tinto, which owns
most of the Oquirrh’s east-facing slopes, to secure agreements and permits to build
more unpaved, soft trails.

(Christopher Cherrington | The Salt Lake Tribune)
Some would come in the near future; others would take decades. Snelgrove said the first
ones are expected to be ready this year or next.

Phase one calls for a 12-mile, single-track trail that would cut through BLM lands, near
Herriman, to Yellow Fork Canyon. The county then would work on another phase in
Butterfield Canyon, linking Rose and Yellow Fork.
“Much of this has been in negotiations,” Snelgrove said, “but all of us at Salt Lake
County and Rio Tinto are operating in good faith and have been good partners to work
with.”
Ritter Canal project

(Francisco Kjolseth | The Salt Lake Tribune) The Kennecott Copper Mine is seen in the
distance Wednesday, March 16, 2022. The west bench near Herriman is expected to
... [truncated — full text is 6539 characters]
